It's a two-player game with two "resources": the sum S and the sum of squares Q. The constraints are cumulative, but each player's move directly affects both sums.

Let's attempt to analyze the game as a "position" with state (S_{n-1}, Q_{n-1}) at the beginning of a turn. The turn number n determines which player moves and which constraint applies.

At odd n: Alice chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that S_{n-1}+x_n ≤ λ n. She wants to win eventually, so she might try to make Q_n = Q_{n-1}+x_n^2 large enough to cause Bazza's next move to be impossible. Thus, the game is about the ability to exceed thresholds. If a player can immediately win by a legal move, they will do so.

Thus, it appears there is a threshold λ0 such that for λ > λ0, Alice wins; for λ < λ0, Bazza wins; and maybe at λ = λ0, the game is a draw (or both have strategies). We need to find λ0. From the simulations, we have:

- For λ = 1, Alice wins.
- For λ = 0.5, Bazza wins.
- For λ = 0.8944 (2/sqrt(5)), we saw that if Alice chooses max on turn 3, she can win if λ > 2/sqrt(5). But that was against Bazza's optimal? Actually, we need to find the exact threshold where the game is balanced.

From the analysis of the first two moves, it seems that Bazza can try to make S_2 as large as possible (subject to his square constraint) to push Alice's next turn over the bound. Conversely, Alice can try to make Q_2 as large as possible on her first turn to push Bazza's next turn over the square bound. So the game is essentially about the "worst-case" values.

Let's formalize the game as a kind of "alternating" game where the state at the beginning of a turn is (S, Q). For odd n, Alice chooses x such that S + x ≤ λ n. She wants to eventually win. For even n, Bazza chooses x such that Q + x^2 ≤ n. He wants to eventually cause Alice to lose. The game ends if a player cannot move.

We can attempt to compute the "critical" λ by considering the "worst-case" for each player assuming optimal play. This is reminiscent of a "strategy" that maintains a certain invariant like S_n = λ n and Q_n = n for all n, but that's only possible for specific λ? Let's examine the invariant: If we have S_n = λ n and Q_n = n for all n, then the constraints hold with equality. But can such a sequence exist? For n odd, Alice's move: she picks x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}. If S_{n-1} = λ (n-1) and n-1 is even, then S_{n-1} = λ (n-1). Then x_n = λ n - λ (n-1) = λ. So she picks x_n = λ. Then Q_n = Q_{n-1} + λ^2. For Q_{n-1} = n-1, we need Q_n = n-1 + λ^2 = n => λ^2 = 1 => λ = 1. So for λ=1, the invariant S_n = n and Q_n = n works. For λ=1, we saw that if both play the "1" strategy, the game goes on forever. So for λ=1, there is a draw strategy? But we earlier found that Alice can win by taking x_1=0 and then x_3=3 - x_2. That suggests that Bazza's "1" strategy is not optimal; he can do better? Actually, we need to see if Bazza has a strategy to survive against Alice's best strategy. In the scenario where Alice takes x_1=0, we saw that Bazza can take x_2= sqrt(2) and then cause Alice to lose on turn 3. But wait, we simulated that: For λ=1, if Alice takes x_1=0, then S_1=0, Q_1=0. Bazza takes x_2= sqrt(2) ≈1.4142, then S_2=1.4142, Q_2=2.0. Then Alice's turn n=3: she needs S_2 ≤ 3. So she can move. She can choose x_3 = 3 - 1.4142 = 1.5858. Then Q_3 = 2 + (1.5858)^2 = 2 + 2.5147 = 4.5147 > 4. Then Bazza on turn 4 cannot move. So Alice wins. But wait, we assumed Bazza chooses x_2 = sqrt(2). But is that allowed? Q_1=0, so he can choose x_2 up to sqrt(2). He chooses sqrt(2). That is legal. Then Alice can win. So if Alice chooses x_1=0, Bazza cannot win by that move; Alice wins on turn 3. But what if Bazza chooses a different x_2? He wants to win; he will choose x_2 to make S_2 > 3? For λ=1, 3λ=3, so he wants S_2 > 3. But S_2 = x_1 + x_2 = x_2 (since x_1=0). He can only choose x_2 up to sqrt(2) ≈1.414, which is less than 3. So he cannot make S_2 > 3. So the only way he could win is to cause Alice to lose on turn 3. But she can always move because S_2 ≤ sqrt(2) < 3. So Alice will have a move. So Bazza cannot win on his second move. Then Alice can win on turn 3 by taking x_3 = 3 - S_2. That yields Q_3 = Q_2 + (3 - S_2)^2. With Q_2 = x_2^2, S_2 = x_2. So Q_3 = x_2^2 + (3 - x_2)^2 = 2x_2^2 - 6x_2 + 9. For x_2 in [0, sqrt(2)], the minimum of Q_3 is at x_2 = 1.5? But 1.5 > sqrt(2), so on [0, sqrt(2)], Q_3 is decreasing? Let's compute: f(x)=2x^2-6x+9. Derivative 4x-6, which is negative for x<1.5. So on [0, sqrt(2)], f is decreasing. Minimum at x=sqrt(2) ≈1.414: f(1.414)=2*2 -6*1.414+9=4-8.484+9=4.516. So Q_3 > 4. So Alice wins. Thus, for λ=1, Alice has a winning strategy: she chooses x_1=0. So Bazza cannot force a win. But we also saw that if Bazza plays the "1" strategy (x_2=1) and Alice plays 1, the game goes on forever. So if Alice plays 1, she doesn't win; but she can deviate to win. So the outcome is that Alice has a winning strategy; the game is not a draw. So for λ=1, Alice wins.

Now, for λ=0.5, we saw that Bazza can win. So there is a threshold λ* between 0.5 and 1 where the outcome changes. We need to find the exact λ*.

From the analysis, it seems that the "critical" λ is the one where there is a "balanced" strategy that allows the game to continue indefinitely, and at that λ, both players can force a draw? But we need to determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all for which Bazza has a winning strategy. Possibly there is a λ such that the game is a draw (neither wins) but the problem asks to determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy. It might be that for some λ, both have winning strategies? That would be a contradiction. Typically, in such games, there is a threshold λ0 such that:

- For λ > λ0, Alice wins.
- For λ < λ0, Bazza wins.
- For λ = λ0, the game is a draw (infinite).

Let's analyze the game more abstractly. Define the state after an even turn 2k: (S_{2k}, Q_{2k}) = (a_k, 2k) if Bazza always plays max. For the game to be infinite, we need that for all k, a_k ≤ λ(2k+1) (so Alice can move) and also that after her move, Q_{2k-1} = 2k-2 + b_k^2, and we need that for all k, Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k? Actually, for Bazza to survive, he needs Q_{2k} ≤ 2k, which is true. But if Q_{2k-1} > 2k, then he loses on his turn. So for infinite play, we need Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k for all k. The fixed point of the recurrence is the solution of b = 2λ - √(2 - b^2). Rearranging: √(2 - b^2) = 2λ - b. Squaring: 2 - b^2 = 4λ^2 - 4λb + b^2 => 2 - b^2 = 4λ^2 - 4λb + b^2 => 2 - b^2 - 4λ^2 + 4λb - b^2 = 0 => 2 - 4λ^2 + 4λb - 2b^2 = 0 => divide 2: 1 - 2λ^2 + 2λb - b^2 = 0 => b^2 - 2λb + (2λ^2 - 1) = 0. So b = λ ± √(λ^2 - (2λ^2 - 1)) = λ ± √(1 - λ^2). So the fixed points are b = λ + √(1 - λ^2) and b = λ - √(1 - λ^2). Note that λ - √(1 - λ^2) is positive for λ > 0? This is a two-player game with perfect information; we need to determine the outcome for each λ. This is a typical "alternating game" where each player tries to force a win. The threshold λ0 is the one where the game is a draw under optimal play. For λ > λ0, Alice can force a win; for λ < λ0, Bazza can force a win; and at λ = λ0, it's a draw. Given the time, perhaps we can find the exact threshold by analyzing the "critical" sequence where the game is infinite. This is a typical "alternating game" and the critical λ is the unique solution to an equation like λ = √(1-λ^2) + λ? No.

Maybe we can consider the game from the perspective of "worst-case" for Alice: she wants to make the bound exceed √2, but Bazza can choose his x_{2k} to minimize her bound. The recurrence b_{k+1} = 2λ - √(2 - b_k^2) is the result of maximal play by both. The condition for her to win is that b_k > √2 for some k. So the threshold is where the sequence b_k just reaches √2. This is a fixed-point condition for the map f(b) = 2λ - √(2 - b^2). The fixed points are b = λ ± √(1-λ^2). For the sequence to touch √2, we need that the fixed point is exactly √2? But that's not possible. So we need to find λ such that there exists some integer n with b_n = √2. This is a problem of "recurrence hitting a boundary". Typically, the threshold is the solution to the equation f(f( ... f(b_1) ...)) = √2. Since b_1 = 3λ - √2, we can try to find λ such that f(b_1) = √2, i.e., 2λ - √(2 - (3λ - √2)^2) = √2. This is the equation we attempted. Let's solve it properly.

We have: 2λ - √(2 - (3λ - √2)^2) = √2. Let’s set u = λ. Then 2u - √2 = √(2 - (3u - √2)^2). Square: (2u - √2)^2 = 2 - (3u - √2)^2. Let's redo the algebra step by step.

Equation: 2λ - √(2 - (3λ - √2)^2) = √2.

Let’s set A = 3λ - √2. Then the equation is 2λ - √(2 - A^2) = √2. Rearr: 2λ - √2 = √(2 - A^2). Square both sides: (2λ - √2)^2 = 2 - A^2. So (4λ^2 - 4√2 λ + 2) = 2 - (3λ - √2)^2. So 4λ^2 - 4√2 λ + 2 = 2 - (9λ^2 - 6√2 λ + 2) = 2 - 9λ^2 + 6√2 λ - 2 = -9λ^2 + 6√2 λ. So we have: 4λ^2 - 4√2 λ + 2 = -9λ^2 + 6√2 λ. Bring all: 4λ^2 + 9λ^2 - 4√2 λ - 6√2 λ + 2 = 0 => 13λ^2 - 10√2 λ + 2 = 0. So λ = [10√2 ± √(200 - 104)]/(26) = [10√2 ± √96]/26 = [10√2 ± 4√6]/26 = (5√2 ± 2√6)/13. Now, for λ = (5√2 + 2√6)/13 ≈ 0.9215, compute the left side of the original equation: 2λ - √(2 - (3λ - √2)^2). - Define the game and the constraints.
- Show that the game ends when a player cannot move; the player who cannot move loses.
- Consider the "maximal play" strategy: each player always chooses the maximum allowed number on their turn. This yields a sequence that can be analyzed.
- Derive recurrence for the sums after even turns.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof. We'll do the following:

- Define the game and the constraints.
- Show that the game is a two-player zero-sum game with perfect information.
- Prove that if λ < (5√2+2√6)/13, then Bazza has a winning strategy. We'll give a strategy: Bazza always plays the maximum possible number on his turns, i.e., x_{2k} = √(2k - Q_{2k-1}). Then we need to show that under this strategy, Alice will eventually be unable to move. This can be done by analyzing the recurrence for the state after even turns. Let's denote s_n = S_n after the n-th move, and q_n = Q_n. We'll show that under this strategy, q_{2k} = 2k, and s_{2k} = s_{2k-2} + x_{2k-1} + √(2 - x_{2k-1}^2). Then we can prove by induction that s_{2k} ≥ k√2. This is true because the minimum of x+√(2-x^2) is √2. So s_{2k} ≥ k√2. Then the condition for Alice to be able to move on turn 2k+1 is s_{2k} ≤ (2k+1)λ. So if for some k, k√2 > (2k+1)λ, then she cannot move. For λ < (5√2+2√6)/13, we need to show that there exists some k such that this inequality holds. But does it hold for all λ below that threshold? We can find the largest λ for which there exists k with k√2 > (2k+1)λ. Since k√2/(2k+1) is increasing, the supremum of these thresholds is the limit √2/2 ≈0.7071. So for λ < 0.7071, we have λ < the limit, so there exists some k such that λ < k√2/(2k+1). For λ between 0.7071 and 0.9215, we have λ > k√2/(2k+1) for all k? Because the maximum of k√2/(2k+1) is √2/2 ≈0.7071. So for λ > 0.7071, we have λ > k√2/(2k+1) for all k. So the condition k√2 > (2k+1)λ is never satisfied. So this approach would only give Bazza win for λ < 0.7071, not up to 0.9215. So the lower bound s_{2k} ≥ k√2 is not tight; the actual s_{2k} can be larger than k√2. So we need to compute the actual value of s_{2k} under the optimal play of Alice to survive. But she can choose her x's to minimize s_{2k}. The minimal possible s_{2k} is k√2. So if λ > 0.7071, then k√2 ≤ (2k+1)λ for all k? Actually, for λ=0.8, we have 2√2/5=0.5657, 3√2/7=0.606, etc. All are less than 0.8, so k√2/(2k+1) < λ for all k. So the inequality k√2 ≤ (2k+1)λ holds for all k. So if Alice plays the minimal strategy (taking x=0 each time), she can survive indefinitely? But we saw that for λ=0.8, with x1=0 and x3=0, she survived turn 5? Actually, we computed for λ=0.8 with x1=0, x3=0, then x4=√2=1.414, S4=2.828, then x5=4-2.828=1.172, Q5=2+1.373=3.373, then x6=√(6-3.373)=√2.627=1.620, S6=2.828+1.172+1.620=5.62, then x7=5.6-5.62=-0.02, so loss. So even with x1=0 and x3=0, she loses. So the minimal s_{2k} is not k√2 because with x1=0 and x3=0, we have s_2=√2, s_4=√2+0+√2=2√2, s_6=2√2+0+√2=3√2? Wait, we computed s_6=5.62, but 3√2=4.2426, so s_6 is larger. So the minimal s_{2k} is not k√2 because the bound on her moves is not zero? Actually, we have to consider the constraints: she can choose x_1=0, then x_3 is constrained by the sum: x_3 ≤ 3λ - S_2 = 2.4-1.414=0.986. If she chooses x_3=0, then S_4 = √2 + 0 + √(4-2)=√2+√2=2√2. 1. Notation: Let x_1, x_2, ... be chosen numbers. Define S_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i, Q_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i^2.
   Alice's move on odd n: S_n ≤ λ n. Bazza's move on even n: Q_n ≤ n.
   The game ends when a player cannot move; the player who cannot move loses.
